  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_c92ad602-3ba7-42d1-b006-58a45618502e'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);

<div id="review_429758825" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/429758825" />
    <a title="Petra-X" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1237196-petra-x"><img alt="Petra-X" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603179398p2/1237196.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/429758825?book_show_action=true">Oct 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Petra-X" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Petra-X" href="/user/show/1237196-petra-x">Petra-X</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=children">children</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer429758825" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14259980662207121793">Marigold loves her daughters a lot. But she loves herself far more. She sees them as children to be formed in her own egotistical image to reflect what she wants the world to think of her. She has one adoring child and one who is beginning to want her own life and not want to look after her mother anymore. Both desperately seek an adequate parent instead of some weird 'best friend' and put their hope in absent and previously unknown fathers. <br/><br/>The mother predictably has a breakdown, predictably ov</span>
  <span id="freeText14259980662207121793" style="display:none">Marigold loves her daughters a lot. But she loves herself far more. She sees them as children to be formed in her own egotistical image to reflect what she wants the world to think of her. She has one adoring child and one who is beginning to want her own life and not want to look after her mother anymore. Both desperately seek an adequate parent instead of some weird 'best friend' and put their hope in absent and previously unknown fathers. <br /><br />The mother predictably has a breakdown, predictably over a man, and the daughters, predictably find that fathers just aren't people you can rely on either. But they have each other and back they go into their old life of looking after mum again.<br /><br />I enjoyed this book, I enjoyed the film too. I wonder what my perspective on it would have been if I'd been between 10 and 15, the target audience? Probably I would have adored the hippie mother rather than despised her selfishness and thought what good kids the daughters were enabling her to behave so badly and dress so well. And just as the author intended I too wouldn't have been able to see the possibly of help that didn't involve losing their ditsy mother. Hindsight is easy, especially with books.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14259980662207121793" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2321756199" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2321756199" />
    <a title="Kiki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4074594-kiki"><img alt="Kiki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545082712p2/4074594.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2321756199?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kiki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kiki" href="/user/show/4074594-kiki">Kiki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2321756199" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4629125255228562416">I'm pretty stunned by the middling rating for this book. Sure, it's a little dated: we don't say "manic depressive" anymore and nor do many of us still see tattoos as particularly shocking (though I can say from experience that being covered in them oftentimes begets loud and unsavoury opinions from strangers). But there's an earnest, powerfully lovable tone to Dolphin's voice, as she struggles so hard to be seen, and later to survive as her life at home spirals completely out of control. She is</span>
  <span id="freeText4629125255228562416" style="display:none">I'm pretty stunned by the middling rating for this book. Sure, it's a little dated: we don't say "manic depressive" anymore and nor do many of us still see tattoos as particularly shocking (though I can say from experience that being covered in them oftentimes begets loud and unsavoury opinions from strangers). But there's an earnest, powerfully lovable tone to Dolphin's voice, as she struggles so hard to be seen, and later to survive as her life at home spirals completely out of control. She is so penned in by misery that she invents wild scenarios and stories inside her own head, idealising every situation and so ending up disappointed when reality proves expectedly ugly.<br /><br />There are layers upon layers to this book that, when I first read it about twelve years ago, I couldn't have picked up on: the pattern of Marigold's depression and mania, her delusions about Micky, her alcoholism; Star desperate to conform, both to protect herself from the viciousness of the uncaring community and to experience the comfort of "normality"; the overwhelming stigma against mental illness in the community; Oliver's mother's circumstantial depression; the emotional abuse inflicted upon Dolphin by her schoolteacher. It's a heavy novel, heavier when you read it again as an adult. I cried multiple times, particularly in those dark moments when Dolphin believes that she is always second best, never good enough, utterly forgotten. It's even more upsetting when we find that she was conceived while Marigold was in a relationship with a man she was using as a substitute for the infamous Micky. Dolphin is a painfully lonely child, so much so that it's often difficult to read. <br /><br />There's a loose, messy ending to this story, a hallmark of Jacqueline Wilson, who writes with incredible and sometimes shocking realism for her young audience. But life is messy, and things don't tie up, and there truly are no happily ever afters. Better this - better that young people understand that what they're striving for is not perfection and undiluted happiness, but to always be moving upwards. Maybe some people don't like that, and maybe they'd rather this was a nicer story. But real life is harder and more terrifying than any make believe. We may as well prepare for it. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4629125255228562416" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1514693284" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1514693284" />
    <a title="Kerri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51649609-kerri"><img alt="Kerri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1573956228p2/51649609.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1514693284?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kerri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kerri" href="/user/show/51649609-kerri">Kerri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385408882" href="/book/show/1413786.The_Illustrated_Mum">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51649609-kerri?shelf=kerri-s-favourites">kerri-s-favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1514693284" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4455482710361438263">🐬<br/> A few days ago I had a spell where I simply could not concentrate on reading. Just over three days. I know because I was keeping track in my journal! Since I read whenever I get the chance, it always throws me off when this happens. I caught up on some episodes of the TV show <i>Heartland</i>, which I love very much, but isn't quite the same. It took me a bit to remember, but generally when this kind of thing happens I reread a much loved book and it kind of restores my brain and gives me back my foc</span>
  <span id="freeText4455482710361438263" style="display:none">🐬<br /> A few days ago I had a spell where I simply could not concentrate on reading. Just over three days. I know because I was keeping track in my journal! Since I read whenever I get the chance, it always throws me off when this happens. I caught up on some episodes of the TV show <i>Heartland</i>, which I love very much, but isn't quite the same. It took me a bit to remember, but generally when this kind of thing happens I reread a much loved book and it kind of restores my brain and gives me back my focus.<br />   Enter Jacqueline Wilson.<br /><br />I've mentioned in quite a few reviews how much I love her, but I'm going to say it again. I love Jacqueline Wilson! I love her new books (there is usually at least one year, often more) and I also love returning to her older ones. <i>The Illustrated Mum</i> is one I especially adore (though I say that about all of them). Star and Dolphin's mother is clearly mentally ill, and as she enters into a manic (or perhaps hypomanic) state, things get quite scary. Star's at the point of disengaging with her mum. She is tired of being the mature and responsible one while Marigold reels from one disaster to another. Dolphin still reveres her mother, though eventually even she sees that things are getting out of hand.<br /><br />Marigold is not a villainous figure, but she is a frustrating one. She rejects hospitals and treatment, traumatised by either past experiences or stories she has heard and absorbed. Both perhaps. This is understandable, but she also had two children in her care, and is often not a fit mother. Both Star and Dolphin are frightened of being put into care, especially because Marigold had such an awful upbringing, which included living in care homes.<br /><br />When Star's father returns, thrilled to discover he has a daughter, dismayed at the state of Marigold, things change quickly, in ways both good and bad.<br /><br />I love Dolphin so much. She is sweet and out of her depth. She is bullied at school, is dyslexic so finds the classroom challenging, and is trying to cope with a difficult situation. She manages to make friends with a boy called Oliver, and their friendship warms my heart.<br />    Star is at an age where she has realised that while their mothers illness is very real, her refusal to address or treat it is causing serious problems that might be avoidable. She is frustrated and upset and I can hardly blame her.<br /><br />I love the way everything is portrayed in this book, and the way the ending feels both hopeful and realistic. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4455482710361438263" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_46252378" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/46252378" />
    <a title="Ed" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/558700-ed"><img alt="Ed" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588901929p2/558700.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/46252378?book_show_action=true">Feb 13, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ed" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ed" href="/user/show/558700-ed">Ed</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385408882" href="/book/show/1413786.The_Illustrated_Mum">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/558700-ed?shelf=re-reads">re-reads</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer46252378" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13980073424595276237">I think this is one of Wilson's darker and more depressing novels, geared toward slightly older children. Marigold is great at getting tattoos, not so great at anything else. Especially naming children - poor Dolphin. Dolphin doesn't even like dolphins. I remember that. The novel focuses on Marigold's spiral into depression, and touches on some sensitive subjects - mental health and depression, alcoholism, learning difficulties, bad parenting... At the age of eleven or whenever I read this book,</span>
  <span id="freeText13980073424595276237" style="display:none">I think this is one of Wilson's darker and more depressing novels, geared toward slightly older children. Marigold is great at getting tattoos, not so great at anything else. Especially naming children - poor Dolphin. Dolphin doesn't even like dolphins. I remember that. The novel focuses on Marigold's spiral into depression, and touches on some sensitive subjects - mental health and depression, alcoholism, learning difficulties, bad parenting... At the age of eleven or whenever I read this book, I couldn't relate to a single one of those issues, but the book still got to me.<br /><br />I found the book quite fascinating. Mental health issues - Marigold, as it turns out, suffers from manic depression - have always held interest with me. And poor awkward little Dolphin turns out to be quite gutsy and resourceful, which is nice. I felt for her. It sure as hell can't be easy dealing with a crazy momma.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13980073424595276237" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_375831435" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/375831435" />
    <a title="Alice Nachir" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11245030-alice-nachir"><img alt="Alice Nachir" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1358360688p2/11245030.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/375831435?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alice Nachir" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alice Nachir" href="/user/show/11245030-alice-nachir">Alice Nachir</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer375831435" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14303215172507678605">The Illustrated Mum by Jacqueline Wilson is a book that really relates to my childhood experiences whilst growing up. Marigold is a mother who is eccentric to say the least; is covered in crazy tattoos, and tends to put the needs of her two daughters Star and Dolphin, last. Her daughters adore her but wish for her to become more 'normal'.<br/> The mood of the book is somewhat depressing yet hopeful with spurts of humour. I can see this book relating to many children coming from broken homes and famil</span>
  <span id="freeText14303215172507678605" style="display:none">The Illustrated Mum by Jacqueline Wilson is a book that really relates to my childhood experiences whilst growing up. Marigold is a mother who is eccentric to say the least; is covered in crazy tattoos, and tends to put the needs of her two daughters Star and Dolphin, last. Her daughters adore her but wish for her to become more 'normal'.<br /> The mood of the book is somewhat depressing yet hopeful with spurts of humour. I can see this book relating to many children coming from broken homes and families. Star is embarrassed by her mother and desperately wants her to change, however Dolphin admires her mother and constantly wants to please her which is extremely difficult. This is due to Marigold having a mental illness as she struggles to cope with her emotions,having severe mood swings. Growing up myself with a mother with a mental illness, I found real comfort in this book that I was not the 'only one'.<br /> Jacqueline Wilson uses much dialogue between characters in this book to convey the messages of a mother living with the responsibility of two daughters while trying to manage a mental disability, and the difficulties of two daughters hoping for change and being strong through everyday family difficulties. Jacqueline Wilson makes the characters come alive so well in this book which is what really reels the reader in. <br />The appropriate age range for this book would be age 10 +. This is due to the book covering many sensitive issues that a younger audience may not be able to connect with. <br />This book would be great for children to read in small groups, where they could pause and discuss feelings and situations of the characters to help them recognize different family situations and increase sensitivity to particular life situations. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14303215172507678605" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_151068584" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/151068584" />
    <a title="Namratha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/114559-namratha"><img alt="Namratha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1406702134p2/114559.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/151068584?book_show_action=true">Feb 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Namratha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Namratha" href="/user/show/114559-namratha">Namratha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385408882" href="/book/show/1413786.The_Illustrated_Mum">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/114559-namratha?shelf=ya-lit">ya-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/114559-namratha?shelf=tween-time">tween-time</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer151068584" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5527445875582628261">First up, I loved the title. I was tickled pink by this fresh badge for a heavily-tattooed individual. It immediately flooded my mind with images of a lean young woman whose pale skin was totally blanketed by inked crosses, cryptic motifs and a host of Celtic symbols.<br/><br/>And the story itself floods you with a bucket load of emotion.<br/><br/>It’s a tale of <b>Marigold</b>; a free-spirited, auburn-haired, rocker-vibe infused, covered-with-tattoos single mother. She has two young daughters Star and Dolphin, both from </span>
  <span id="freeText5527445875582628261" style="display:none">First up, I loved the title. I was tickled pink by this fresh badge for a heavily-tattooed individual. It immediately flooded my mind with images of a lean young woman whose pale skin was totally blanketed by inked crosses, cryptic motifs and a host of Celtic symbols.<br /><br />And the story itself floods you with a bucket load of emotion.<br /><br />It’s a tale of <b>Marigold</b>; a free-spirited, auburn-haired, rocker-vibe infused, covered-with-tattoos single mother. She has two young daughters Star and Dolphin, both from different fathers. Marigold sounds like a free-thinker’s edgy dream. You expect the story to be about a fiercely independent heroine who fights against a disapproving society so that she can raise her daughters in the way that she wants them to be raised. You expect the pages to be bursting with flower-power and righteous indignation.<br /><br />Sadly, life is rarely a walk down a garden path.<br /><br />Marigold suffers from <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmedhealth/PMH0001924/" rel="nofollow noopener">Manic-Depression</a>. She refuses to believe that she has a problem and the possibility of taking treatment is a non-existent option. Star and Dolphin have become accustomed to their mother’s weird spells. But getting used to something does not necessarily mean that you are resigned to it and willing to accept it with a weary roll of your eyes. Marigold parties hard, gets herself inked at regular intervals, pulls all-nighters with random men, drinks indiscriminately and leaves behind a sorry train-wreck for her daughters.<br /><br /><b>Star</b>, the elder daughter, used to love her mother’s uniqueness but as she grows up, she seeks the safety of conformity. She cannot excuse Marigold’s "episodes" anymore and just wants a normal life. <b>Dolphin</b>, on the other hand, still loves her mother to distraction. She adores her brightly coloured, <i>covered-like-a-comic-strip</i> parent. She cherishes the little gestures Marigold makes in her brief moments of sanity. Dolphin loves Star too and though the sibling affection is reciprocated, things will soon come to an ugly head. <b>
  <u>The Illustrated Mum</u>
</b> is a book that explains Dolphin’s journey of trying to be an adult in a world that is unpredictable and at times downright scary.<br /><br /><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jacqueline_Wilson" rel="nofollow noopener">Jacqueline Wilson</a> is a children’s author who has always been credited for taking up serious issues in a child’s life and handling it with a winning combination of sensitivity and humour. In <b>
  <u>The Illustrated Mum</u>
</b>, she tackles a grave issue and leaves you with a sizeable knot in your throat. The comical quips were few, but the author makes you ache for Dolphin and her earnestness. <br /><br />Wilson’s preteen heroines are usually full-of-beans and ready to take on the world amidst their own private hells. Dolphin is no different. She raises all your dormant maternal instincts and makes you want to crush her in a big, squishy comforting hug. My only grouse was with the abrupt ending. I didn’t particular appreciate the convenient tying up of odd ends. It felt like a cop-out.<br /><br />And yet, you clamber on till the end since you want a happy-ending for Dolphin.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5527445875582628261" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1791278624" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1791278624" />
    <a title="Codie Wright" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21253712-codie-wright"><img alt="Codie Wright" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595461941p2/21253712.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1791278624?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Codie Wright" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Codie Wright" href="/user/show/21253712-codie-wright">Codie Wright</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1791278624" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16839956267390512200">So, the story in a nutshell, follows Dolphin (yep, that’s her name…), and her sister Star (which is a name that doesn’t actually seem that bizarre in comparison to some children I’ve met…) who live with their mother Marigold who is clearly suffering from some sort of manic depression throughout the story. Star’s Dad Micky comes on to the scene and whisks Star (who is the parental figure to both Dolphin and her mum, Marigold) away, leaving Dolphin dealing her poorly mum as well as school bullies </span>
  <span id="freeText16839956267390512200" style="display:none">So, the story in a nutshell, follows Dolphin (yep, that’s her name…), and her sister Star (which is a name that doesn’t actually seem that bizarre in comparison to some children I’ve met…) who live with their mother Marigold who is clearly suffering from some sort of manic depression throughout the story. Star’s Dad Micky comes on to the scene and whisks Star (who is the parental figure to both Dolphin and her mum, Marigold) away, leaving Dolphin dealing her poorly mum as well as school bullies and trying to look after herself, despite being only 11. <br /><br />There are some really dark points in this book that I hadn’t noticed, or fully appreciated as a child. For example, Marigold steals, or at least has in the past stolen, some credit cards. Also, Dolphin shows some traits of Obsessive Compulsive Disorder - presumably the only way she can find control in her life. <br /><br />It was also quite difficult to read as my Mum suffered with depression when I was a child, so some parts were particularly relatable (although thankfully not all!). In fact, I think it was a frighteningly real portrayal of a parent who is suffering with their mental health and a very brave, but important, risk to take for a children’s book. Spoiler alert: There is no dramatic “happy ending”, where everything is suddenly okay again, and I personally appreciated that. It’s not how life works and although the ending is upbeat and optimistic, it’s also realistic and doesn’t patronise the children reading the book. I think that’s so important. <br /><br />There was a moment, when Oliver and Dolphin call up a leisure centre and just so happen to manage to get in touch with Dolphin’s Dad, who she has never met, which did feel a bit too easy and very unrealistic. Aside from that, it was easy to forget that I was reading a book that is intended for children, such is Wilson’s writing. It really is a timeless book that I absolutely loved re-reading and I’m excited to start the next one already!</span>
  <a data-text-id="16839956267390512200" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer207423128" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1651675236836303377">We always love our mums, don't we? Star and Dolphin definitely love their mum, Marigold, though she's a difficult mum to love. For starters, she's covered head to toe in strange tattoos, and, second, she's not always there for her daughters. Such an eccentric mum makes life complicated and disturbing for the two girls. When Marigold comes home from a rock concert with Star's dad, Micky (not Dolphin's dad---he is a different Micky, it turns out), life becomes much more complicated and disturbing.</span>
  <span id="freeText1651675236836303377" style="display:none">We always love our mums, don't we? Star and Dolphin definitely love their mum, Marigold, though she's a difficult mum to love. For starters, she's covered head to toe in strange tattoos, and, second, she's not always there for her daughters. Such an eccentric mum makes life complicated and disturbing for the two girls. When Marigold comes home from a rock concert with Star's dad, Micky (not Dolphin's dad---he is a different Micky, it turns out), life becomes much more complicated and disturbing. <br /><br />An unexpectedly complex look at a complex person and her complex sister and her complex mother. Recommended.</span>

    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20608600-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601760778p2/20608600.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1539158200?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/20608600-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20608600-laura?shelf=five-star">five-star</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1539158200" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1293192224418938061">This is possibly one of my very favourite Wilson books. Like most of her books, I read it many times as a child. Looking back as an adult, it's clear the mother is living with some form of mental illness, most likely bipolar disorder. <br/><br/>As a child reading this, and from the perspective of the child from which this book is written, all you understand is that Mum is quite impulsive and a little bit weird. She isn't like a normal mum, there's often little money to buy the things the two daughters (Do</span>
  <span id="freeText1293192224418938061" style="display:none">This is possibly one of my very favourite Wilson books. Like most of her books, I read it many times as a child. Looking back as an adult, it's clear the mother is living with some form of mental illness, most likely bipolar disorder. <br /><br />As a child reading this, and from the perspective of the child from which this book is written, all you understand is that Mum is quite impulsive and a little bit weird. She isn't like a normal mum, there's often little money to buy the things the two daughters (Dolphin and Star) need. Mum is instead very good at telling stories and coming up with fun things to do. As with all mental illness, living with someone affected has its downsides.<br /><br />I was impressed on how Wilson challenged the stereotypes surrounding mental health issues. Mum isn't crazy, she's just wired differently. That doesn't make her a bad person does it?<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1293192224418938061" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2178905093" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2178905093" />
    <a title="Nav (she/her) &amp;#x1f327;" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68484644-nav-she-her-x1f327"><img alt="Nav (she/her) &amp;#x1f327;" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598893622p2/68484644.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780440865452" href="/book/show/3290700-the-illustrated-mum">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68484644-nav-she-her-x1f327?shelf=middle-grade">middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68484644-nav-she-her-x1f327?shelf=general-fiction">general-fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2178905093" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7579758873712430962">The Illustrated Mum follows the life of a young girl called Dolphin who lives with her older sister Star and mother Marigold who is currently suffering with mental health issues.<br/><br/>I actually enjoyed this book a lot more than I was expecting to but I think that is just a testament to how good Jacqueline Wilsons' writing is.<br/><br/>I began to care for all three characters really early on and I really wanted a happy ending for Dolphin.<br/><br/>I liked Dolphin's friendship with Oliver and it was good to see that she s</span>
  <span id="freeText7579758873712430962" style="display:none">The Illustrated Mum follows the life of a young girl called Dolphin who lives with her older sister Star and mother Marigold who is currently suffering with mental health issues.<br /><br />I actually enjoyed this book a lot more than I was expecting to but I think that is just a testament to how good Jacqueline Wilsons' writing is.<br /><br />I began to care for all three characters really early on and I really wanted a happy ending for Dolphin.<br /><br />I liked Dolphin's friendship with Oliver and it was good to see that she stuck to her guns and decided to be friends with a less popular child over the girls who were popular but mean.<br /><br />The storyline felt real and believable and I feel Jacqueline Wilson wrote about difficult topics such as mental health issues in a respectful and hopeful manner.<br /><br />I feel there are many young people who will be able to relate to Dolphin, in particular to the experiences that Dolphin went through at school (being picked on) and also how she felt left out when Mickey came along.<br /><br />The relationship between Dolphin and Michael was nice to see and I actually liked how it started off a bit awkward to begin with.<br /><br />The ending seemed hopeful and I was glad everyone made up in the end. But I would have loved to have known what happened next and whether Dolphin stayed in touch with Michael.<br /><br />All in all, I really enjoyed The Illustrated Mum and I would definitely recommend it to younger readers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7579758873712430962" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1655982693" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1655982693" />
    <a title="Kate (Lillytales)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30538697-kate-lillytales"><img alt="Kate (Lillytales)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1463014708p2/30538697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1655982693?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kate (Lillytales)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kate (Lillytales)" href="/user/show/30538697-kate-lillytales">Kate (Lillytales)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30538697-kate-lillytales?shelf=2016-reading-challenge">2016-reading-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1655982693" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18350373503378612448">Jacqueline Wilson was one of my all-time favourite authors during childhood and I picked up the Illustrated Mum again as a bit of an experiment. I wanted to read it from an adult perspective as I remember being a little upset by it as a child. The Illustrated Mum is the story of two sisters, Dolphin and Star, and their Mum Marigold. Marigold isn't a 'typical' Mum as she struggles to form a parent/child relationship with the girls. Dolphin and Star spend a lot of time worrying about Marigold as s</span>
  <span id="freeText18350373503378612448" style="display:none">Jacqueline Wilson was one of my all-time favourite authors during childhood and I picked up the Illustrated Mum again as a bit of an experiment. I wanted to read it from an adult perspective as I remember being a little upset by it as a child. The Illustrated Mum is the story of two sisters, Dolphin and Star, and their Mum Marigold. Marigold isn't a 'typical' Mum as she struggles to form a parent/child relationship with the girls. Dolphin and Star spend a lot of time worrying about Marigold as she drinks a bit too much, continues to spend money they don't have on tattoos and she is continuously searching to reclaim her relationship with Star's father, Mickey. <br /><br />I think it's fantastic how Jacqueline Wilson gives children a different perspective on family structures. A common theme throughout all of her books is the idea of diverse families with real-life problems. I think it's really important for children to have access to a diverse range of stories surrounding family and Wilson's books are a vital role in showing that growing up isn't always peachy keen. Sometimes children are forced unwillingly into adult roles and this is a story of love, endurance and the power of empathy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18350373503378612448" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1640317347" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1640317347" />
    <a title="Chetana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4622325-chetana"><img alt="Chetana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1293013158p2/4622325.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1640317347?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chetana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chetana" href="/user/show/4622325-chetana">Chetana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1640317347" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14512667063063712677">Re-read this book for the nth time. That Jacqueline Wilson is such an underrated children's book author, continues to surprise me. Her depiction of dysfunctional, broken families - in this book, it was that the tattooed single mum who is manic depressive but very loving - is refreshingly honest and real. Though these families are extremes, the emotional roller coaster that they encounter is so familiar. Whether it's a neat four member-ed unit or a mother-daughter duo, families _are_ wrung with c</span>
  <span id="freeText14512667063063712677" style="display:none">Re-read this book for the nth time. That Jacqueline Wilson is such an underrated children's book author, continues to surprise me. Her depiction of dysfunctional, broken families - in this book, it was that the tattooed single mum who is manic depressive but very loving - is refreshingly honest and real. Though these families are extremes, the emotional roller coaster that they encounter is so familiar. Whether it's a neat four member-ed unit or a mother-daughter duo, families _are_ wrung with conflicting feelings of selfishness, irritation, (lack of) respect and love towards one another; it's a reality that's rarely found in a children's book. Giving this a five for both the story and Wilson.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14512667063063712677" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jade Singleton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jade Singleton" href="/user/show/12641093-jade-singleton">Jade Singleton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer409837172" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3277709959759939461">The Illustrated Mum is the story of ten year old Dolphin and her life living with Mum, Marigold and older sister, Star.<br/><br/>Marigold is not your average Mum. She does not work, is covered in tattoos and likes to party. Marigold, Dolphin and Star live together in a run-down flat and the story, which is told by Dolphin, describes her life growing up. Dolphin is very naive and although she realises that her mother is different to others, she always sees the good side. She is very innocent compared to he</span>
  <span id="freeText3277709959759939461" style="display:none">The Illustrated Mum is the story of ten year old Dolphin and her life living with Mum, Marigold and older sister, Star.<br /><br />Marigold is not your average Mum. She does not work, is covered in tattoos and likes to party. Marigold, Dolphin and Star live together in a run-down flat and the story, which is told by Dolphin, describes her life growing up. Dolphin is very naive and although she realises that her mother is different to others, she always sees the good side. She is very innocent compared to her older sister,Star. Star is a young teen with little patience for her Mum's behaviour. She often argues with Marigold, and Dolphin often feels caught in the middle.<br /><br />I have read this book as a child and i'm now reading it again. I love this book as it is so easy to develop Dolphin's character in your mind and fall in love with her innocence. The book really enables you to see beyond a typical middle-class family, and demonstrates how important a family bond is no matter what background.<br /><br />I would recommend this book for children aged 10-12 and would suggest it for independent reading in a year six classroom. I do think however, that this book for appeal more to girls. <br /><br />This book is a great read and will evoke laughter and maybe even tears.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer18405184234422018393">I enjoyed this it was an easy book to read. I'll be studying this on my children's literature course and I think this is a great book for children to read in the respect that they can learn a lot about functional families, the way kids are treated at school, and question what a good parent is. However I don't know if younger children would fully understand that the mother has manic depression. There is also a mention of Dolphin having Dyslexia and it doesn't really go into depth about what this </span>
  <span id="freeText18405184234422018393" style="display:none">I enjoyed this it was an easy book to read. I'll be studying this on my children's literature course and I think this is a great book for children to read in the respect that they can learn a lot about functional families, the way kids are treated at school, and question what a good parent is. However I don't know if younger children would fully understand that the mother has manic depression. There is also a mention of Dolphin having Dyslexia and it doesn't really go into depth about what this means and how she struggles with this compared to other children I would have liked to have seen that explored more. Also the ending is very unresolved we don't know where the daughters end up throughout it all which I personally would have liked to have seen. Overall it was a good children's book though.</span>

<div id="review_651812004" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/651812004" />
    <a title="Devon Flaherty" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17163060-devon-flaherty"><img alt="Devon Flaherty" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442875304p2/17163060.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/651812004?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Devon Flaherty" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Devon Flaherty" href="/user/show/17163060-devon-flaherty">Devon Flaherty</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer651812004" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9287707830875552430">....<br/><br/>I had a week before me, during which I was planning and packing for a very big trip, two thick books lined up in the queue (which would be the wooden box between my front door and the couch), and Dustbin Baby already read. I was not that impressed, and took a stroll through the giant spreadsheet of my Best Books to Read, where I found a total of twelve more Jacqueline Wilson books. I was not pumped. But I was surprised.<br/><br/>Who is this Jacqueline Wilson character that she makes the top 1200 (or s</span>
  <span id="freeText9287707830875552430" style="display:none">....<br /><br />I had a week before me, during which I was planning and packing for a very big trip, two thick books lined up in the queue (which would be the wooden box between my front door and the couch), and Dustbin Baby already read. I was not that impressed, and took a stroll through the giant spreadsheet of my Best Books to Read, where I found a total of twelve more Jacqueline Wilson books. I was not pumped. But I was surprised.<br /><br />Who is this Jacqueline Wilson character that she makes the top 1200 (or so) books thirteen times and yet ceases to impress me? I’ll tell you. She’s a British lady. She has written over sixty-one YA books. She is now some sort of tour de force, complete with a whole online wonderland of games and information, as well as more than a few books-to-movies. She became popular after something like thirty books, when she wrote The Story of Tracy Beaker. Her popularity is thanks almost entirely to the people of England. And here is her schtick: since the 70s, she has been writing short, accessible, encouraging yet realistic books about kids and teens in really tough situations. I ended up gobbling down six more Wilson’s books (that’s all my American library had) before laying out my judgement, and the topics that sampling covered (some of her most lauded, included) were being a foster care child, having a parent with a mental disorder, dealing with the death of a friend, going through parental divorce, being a twin, and being abandoned by your parents.<br /><br />I can’t help but keep thinking about the comment I received about my book from Zack Smith at the local paper: “I’m just going to say it: More coming-of-age novels need stuff like this. Estranged parents and soured relationships can only take you so far.” And yet, I think that Wilson’s books, with their accessibility and their very real and positive outlook, are also important to children. And I don’t know who benefits more: kids who can relate to this stuff, or kids who can learn compassion and understanding. (On the other hand, I think romps into light, airy, and imaginary worlds is also good food for the developing mind.)<br /><br />These are the books I read (some of them in an afternoon), in this order:<br /><br /> Dustbin Baby, 2001<br /> The Illustrated Mum, 1999<br /> Double Act, 1995<br /> The Story of Tracy Beaker, 1991<br /> The Suitcase Kid, 1992<br /> Vicky Angel, 2001<br /> Girls in Tears, 2002<br /><br />In the end, these books are not really my cup o’ tea, although there was some fun in breezing through them and analyzing them together. And really, I can see the merits of them. Really. Writing-wise, they were unspectacular. Wilson sticks to such straight-forward vocabulary that it can grow flat. At no time was I transported to a grassy hillside. While her characters are rounded out nicely, her plots (I think because of the featureless writing style) leave you wishing for more. With all these terrible, real-life situations and triumphant endings, I ended the last page of the last book wondering why my heart had not soared nor one lonely tear come to my eye. However, the ideas behind the books are solid, and the insight, empathy, and tact with which she writes about them are spot-on. She understands kids–and these kinds of kids (/preteens/teens)–unlike any other author (or even person) I have ever read (met).<br /><br />Six random observations:<br /><br />One, I absolutely love the way so many of her main characters see themselves in such negative terms (including, of course, their appearance, but also often their behavior) or even just unrealistic terms, but Wilson never takes the easy way out explaining and patronizing about how they really are beautiful or slender or kind or whatever. The character always slowly, and in a very understated way, discovers something redeemable about themselves, for themselves, and with the tender help of one compassionate person. We need not cue the sappy music.<br /><br />Two, these books are for girls. Out of the seven I read, not one of them would be of too much interest to a boy. They were all about girls and written to girls. That’s all.<br /><br />Three, part of how Wilson does her Wilson-thing is using illustrations (by Nick Sharrat) and other story-telling devices. Yeah, lots of YA books these days use them, too. Like Captain Underpants and Origami Yoda. Wilson uses different techniques in each book, like telling the story through a journal, or starting each chapter with a letter of the alphabet, or hanging the storyline in the structure of a walk through town. Got it?<br /><br />Four, I also love that she is honest about how kids perspectives are often skewed, especially in that they are often attached where it is unsafe and repelled where it would be good. So many times, her heroine clung to an unhealthy relationship because it was familiar, justifying behaviors and running an internal dialogue of excuses, or separated themselves unwisely from people that could have offered them much better than they were already getting. We adults could take a hint, too.<br /><br />Five, occasionally Wilson’s books bring up the occult or witchcraft, and not necessarily in a fantasy way. I believe she regards it as a type of play-acting, but the line is a thin one. Several of her characters imagine themselves using magic to change things around them or even hurt others. One of the characters imagines a friend who is an “ace” at the occult (turning into a vampire, etc.). Also, Christian belief is seen more in negative terms. The kid characters tend to view religion as worthless and even mock it, while the only positive religious character I read in Wilson’s books was sort of flaky about her religious affiliation to the point that you eventually forgot she was a Christian. (I only just now remembered her because the description of her stiff collar clashing with her lively pants was a stand-out.)<br /><br />Six, there are movies. I have not seen any of them yet, and have no idea about their availability stateside, but I plan to look into it.<br /><br />The Illustrated Mum is a fascinating book for me, not least of all because I too have a family member with bipolar disorder. I found it to be part of the new language and dialogue about this and similar illnesses, where they focus more on the positive elements of the bipolar (or autistic or ADHD or whatever) personality. Being as that may, the mother was an entrancing character, and the struggles of the family so honest and accurate. Besides the slightly cheesy last paragraph, I found this book much better than the first.<br /><br />....<br /><br />REVIEW FROM THE STARVING ARTIST</span>
  <a data-text-id="9287707830875552430" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText17938495411745043232" style="display:none">Dolphin and Star’s mum is rather egotistical and shapes the way her children think to fit the way that she sees herself. Long-lost fathers add an element of suspense to the story . After a search for her old ex-boyfriend , their mum reunited with ‘Micky’ , a guy who she went to concerts with ,10 odd years ago. Star finds out that her real father IS Micky , and leaves with him. After breaking up with Micky, she breaks down and thinks that it is because she doesn’t look like the woman that he wants . Left with only her mother in the flat , Dolphin wishes star hadn’t left so selfishly . She feels helpless and alone . One night , dolphin couldn’t sleep , and went to the kitchen for a sip of water . She heard someone moving upstairs , and assumed that it was just her mother using the bathroom . She goes upstairs to find her mother covered in semi-dried white paint .she has to try to find her father . Her mother is out in a psychiatric ward.</span>

<div id="review_3479239451" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3479239451" />
    <a title="Miceál" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/106758112-mice-l"><img alt="Miceál" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576326199p2/106758112.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780440867814" href="/book/show/40186731-the-illustrated-mum">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106758112-mice-l?shelf=childhood-faves">childhood-faves</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3479239451" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10576665299436287635">Ooh man, this one hits harder now I reread it as an adult. It always struck me in a certain way when I read it as a child, because I suppose I was subconsciously picking up on themes that were relevant to my life but I just didn't understand it yet. Now I'm older, I'm struck by the similarities between Star and Dolphin's experiences and my own. When I read the book as a child I was very much of Dolphin's opinion when it came to Marigold; now I'm an adult I vibe much more with Star. It just goes </span>
  <span id="freeText10576665299436287635" style="display:none">Ooh man, this one hits harder now I reread it as an adult. It always struck me in a certain way when I read it as a child, because I suppose I was subconsciously picking up on themes that were relevant to my life but I just didn't understand it yet. Now I'm older, I'm struck by the similarities between Star and Dolphin's experiences and my own. When I read the book as a child I was very much of Dolphin's opinion when it came to Marigold; now I'm an adult I vibe much more with Star. It just goes to show how spot-on this is when it comes to how children of different ages view such things.<br /><br />I've always admired Jacqueline Wilson for how she approaches heavy subjects in her books. When I was a child I was never a fan of easy stories; I wanted to see myself represented, and I wanted to see kids being brave and resourceful and not having all that many adults to rely on. I found a lot of that in Jacqueline's books, and reading this back as an adult I can see how I internalised many lessons from it. It was very comforting, even when I didn't fully understand what was going on, to see a mother who acted like mine did -- and to see characters who had the same strange fears as I did. It was something I couldn't explain to my friends. They saw my mother acting normally, and if she wasn't being totally normal, they saw her as being a cool, fun mum. Only I really understand what certain tones of voice meant; only I felt the fear when she would get an idea into her head and I knew it wouldn't be a good one. It's a very specific thing, when you're the child parenting your parent, and this book represented it so well. As a child it made me feel very capable and brave; as an adult it made me feel even more proud of my child self, but sad, too. Some parts of this were very heavy to read. They hit very close to home.<br /><br />Jacqueline Wilson's books are so important, and I think every child would benefit from reading them. Nothing brings this home more than rereading as an adult -- they're just as enjoyable to read when older, and somehow the simplicity of the writing loses none of its impact. I'm sure I'm not the only one who feels a sense of nostalgia in coming back to these books, either. When I think back to all the early times when I realised that perhaps my experiences weren't something that happened only to me, Jacqueline Wilson's books -- and especially this one -- feature prominently.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10576665299436287635" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_67924987" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/67924987" />

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/229988-jenn?shelf=kid-at-heart">kid-at-heart</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/229988-jenn?shelf=audio">audio</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer67924987&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer67924987" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13463726025945115071">Maybe I'm just way too cynical but I kinda resented the last quarter of this book. The story started off brutally honest, real, uncomfortable, and a bit sad. Two daughters, one of them a teen called Star, the other younger and called Dolphin (Dol), live with their mother, Marigold, who is mentally ill. Because the reader knows this right off the bat, it is difficult to hate her or see her as the villain. For awhile, honestly, there is no real villain except for the community as a whole who does </span>
  <span id="freeText13463726025945115071" style="display:none">Maybe I'm just way too cynical but I kinda resented the last quarter of this book. The story started off brutally honest, real, uncomfortable, and a bit sad. Two daughters, one of them a teen called Star, the other younger and called Dolphin (Dol), live with their mother, Marigold, who is mentally ill. Because the reader knows this right off the bat, it is difficult to hate her or see her as the villain. For awhile, honestly, there is no real villain except for the community as a whole who does absolutely nothing to help the mother or her daughters. There's the horrible neighbor who threatens and berates, the kids at school who tease mercilessly and their worthless teacher who may as well be one of her students for all the good she does. Star's father turns up and we can't even hate him because this is the first he's heard of Star. He assumes responsibility for her immediately and attempts to get her away from her crazy, tattooed mum. She welcomes this and runs off to live with him. <br /><br />Poor Dolphin is left to take care of Marigold. She has a breakdown one night and Dolphin, innocently, calls for help and Marigold is shipped off to the hospital leaving Dolphin with nowhere to go. And here is where the story turns into a ridiculous fairytale which didn't sit right with me. <br /><br />I listened to the audiobook version which I would recommend. There is a charm to the writing and I did find myself rooting for Dolphin, Star, and even Marigold. Marigold has it rough. Her deck of cards sucks and she's doing the best she can. As are her daughters. When it became more of a fairytale story, I didn't need to root anymore and that was disappointing. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13463726025945115071" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_884543050" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Shajeda Begum" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29741686-shajeda-begum"><img alt="Shajeda Begum" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/884543050?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shajeda Begum" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shajeda Begum" href="/user/show/29741686-shajeda-begum">Shajeda Begum</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer884543050" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10545165158727025713">I read this book when I was around 10 years old and I personally loved this book. The story explores different things children experience as they are growing up. The mother Marigold is covered in tattoos and she always puts her two daughters: Star and Dolphin last. She is not an attentive mother and she is quite self centred. Her daughters wish for their mother to be normal. The book is quite gloomy however there are many instances where there is humour. This book touches on social issues such a</span>
  <span id="freeText10545165158727025713" style="display:none">I read this book when I was around 10 years old and I personally loved this book. The story explores different things children experience as they are growing up. The mother Marigold is covered in tattoos and she always puts her two daughters: Star and Dolphin last. She is not an attentive mother and she is quite self centred. Her daughters wish for their mother to be normal. The book is quite gloomy however there are many instances where there is humour. This book touches on social issues such as loneliness; it explores the life of a single mother therefore a lot of children can relate to Star and Dolphin's story. Star is embarrassed by her mothers actions but Dolphin admires her mother a lot and is constantly trying to please her. Marigold's actions are due to her mental illness therefore she battles with her inner self and her emotions tend to become quite dysfunctional. The characters in this book portray a realistic lifestyle and many children can relate to it. This chapter book is suitable for children in KS2 who are comfortable with chapter books. However it is more appropriate for children year 5 + because the themes explored in this book are sensitive. The book could be on a shelf in the classroom or the library for children to read independently during reading time or they could take it home to read over the weekend. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10545165158727025713" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3344820612" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3344820612" />
    <a title="Eustacia Tan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6609117-eustacia-tan"><img alt="Eustacia Tan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553435967p2/6609117.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3344820612?book_show_action=true">May 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eustacia Tan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eustacia Tan" href="/user/show/6609117-eustacia-tan">Eustacia Tan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6609117-eustacia-tan?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6609117-eustacia-tan?shelf=childhood-favourites">childhood-favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3344820612" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11671266744338671826">This is a book that I’ve reread quite a few times over the years and I knew it was dark, but wow this was dark. Marigold, the illustrated Mum, has bipolar disorder, something which is clear to the reader but pretty much unknown to the girls until the end of the book. All they know is that their mum is sometimes down, sometimes up, sometimes very fun, and sometimes absolutely scary.<br/><br/>When I was younger, I remember thinking that Marigold seemed pretty fun (like Dolphin’s friend Oliver!) and her brea</span>
  <span id="freeText11671266744338671826" style="display:none">This is a book that I’ve reread quite a few times over the years and I knew it was dark, but wow this was dark. Marigold, the illustrated Mum, has bipolar disorder, something which is clear to the reader but pretty much unknown to the girls until the end of the book. All they know is that their mum is sometimes down, sometimes up, sometimes very fun, and sometimes absolutely scary.<br /><br />When I was younger, I remember thinking that Marigold seemed pretty fun (like Dolphin’s friend Oliver!) and her breakdown was frightening. Now, the signs that Marigold is rather unstable are very clear and I find myself empathising with older sister Star a lot more – all she wants is to be normal after years of having to act as the parent to her mother and younger sister. It’s a heavy responsibility and one that she absolutely doesn’t want (and shouldn’t have to shoulder).<br /><br />But sympathetic as Star was, Dolphin was definitely the perfect main character and narrator for the book. She loves both her sister and her mother and her eyes are blinded just enough that Marigold comes across as a magical mum until she can no longer ignore the truth. Her story is powerful and even though I’ve reread this many times, I still feel her fear very strongly.<br /><br />Full review at <a href="https://eustaciatan.com/2020/05/daily-reading-18th-may-the-illustrated-mum.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Eustea Reads</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="11671266744338671826" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I loved how each chapter was named after one of Marigold's tatoos and each tatoo had some significance to that part of the story and Dolphin's life with her mum or her sister, Star.   <span id="freeText8390913514603060925" style="display:none">This book is pushing a five star rating. I really loved it. I loved how each chapter was named after one of Marigold's tatoos and each tatoo had some significance to that part of the story and Dolphin's life with her mum or her sister, Star. I thought the author's portrayal of this type of mental illness through the character of Marigold was very accurate. This is a serious subject and a serious story, but Wilson makes it very accessible to a younger audience, probably 11 and up I'd say. I loved the illustrations throughout the book. A great read, I didn't ever want to stop reading it :) p.s. In hindsight I've decided to give it five stars. That last little star has been haunting me. </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer249091323" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5329211709620921087">This is one of the saddest, most heartfelt books I've ever read. <br/>IT totally transforms you to a different state of mind, and Jaqueline Wilson writes with such careful carelessness that she might as well be writing about Little Red Riding Hood whereas one might think that the depth and gravity of The Illustrated Mum would require a much more emotional writing style.<br/>Overall the effect is astounding. I never expected to like her book this much. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('CjXq2HJEIopjNQW7kPf3/BcRKT3dTsp539yg0RGazjpRH4S9n6FOBsG7iwzAacLSOFKKu/U/jwiQMVyuwHqCng==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('PEwm3n94lUuA1GwVB7dwTg/JZBtGX7bNDovmUfZYQLBnZki7kp35xyJa4qJXKUVgIIrHnW4u87xBZhouJ7gMFA==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/22602.Jacqueline_Wilson">About Jacqueline Wilson</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/22602.Jacqueline_Wilson"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1373964706p3/22602.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/22602.Jacqueline_Wilson">Jacqueline Wilson
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
3,363
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16650449843981310309">Jacqueline Wilson was born in Bath in 1945, but spent most of her childhood in Kingston-on-Thames. She always wanted to be a writer and wrote her first ‘novel’ when she was nine, filling in countless Woolworths’ exercise books as she grew up. As a teenager she started work for a magazine publishing company and then went on to work as a journalist on Jackie magazine (which she was told was named af</span>
  <span id="freeText16650449843981310309" style="display:none">Jacqueline Wilson was born in Bath in 1945, but spent most of her childhood in Kingston-on-Thames. She always wanted to be a writer and wrote her first ‘novel’ when she was nine, filling in countless Woolworths’ exercise books as she grew up. As a teenager she started work for a magazine publishing company and then went on to work as a journalist on Jackie magazine (which she was told was named after her!) before turning to writing novels full-time.<br /><br />One of Jacqueline’s most successful and enduring creations has been the famous Tracy Beaker, who first appeared in 1991 in The Story of Tracy Beaker. This was also the first of her books to be illustrated by Nick Sharratt. Since then Jacqueline has been on countless awards shortlists and has gone on to win many awards. The Illustrated Mum won the Guardian Children’s Fiction Award, the 1999 Children’s Book of the Year at the British Book Awards and was also shortlisted for the 1999 Whitbread Children’s Book Award.<br /><br />Double Act won the prestigious Smarties Medal and the Children’s Book Award as well as being highly commended for the Carnegie Medal. The Story of Tracy Beaker won the 2002 Blue Peter People’s Choice Award.<br /><br />Jacqueline is one of the nation’s favourite authors, and her books are loved and cherished by young readers not only in the UK but all over the world. She has sold millions of books and in the UK alone the total now stands at over 35 million!<br /><br />In 2002 Jacqueline was awarded the OBE for services to literacy in schools and from 2005 to 2007 she was the Children’s Laureate. In 2008 she became Dame Jacqueline Wilson.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16650449843981310309" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
